<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The C-Fern ( Ceratopteris richardii ) genome: insights into plant genome evolution with the first partial homosporous fern genome assembly"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2019 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-12-03"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Ferns are notorious for possessing large genomes and numerous chromosomes. Despite decades of speculation, the processes underlying the expansive genomes of ferns are unclear, largely due to the absence of a sequenced homosporous fern genome. The lack of this crucial resource has not only hindered investigations of evolutionary processes responsible for the unusual genome characteristics of homosporous ferns, but also impeded synthesis of genome evolution across land plants. Here, we used the model fern species Ceratopteris richardii to address the processes (e.g., polyploidy, spread of repeat elements) by which the large genomes and high chromosome numbers typical of homosporous ferns may have evolved and have been maintained. We directly compared repeat compositions in species spanning the green plant tree of life and a diversity of genome sizes, as well as both short- and long-read-based assemblies of Ceratopteris. We found evidence consistent with a single ancient polyploidy event in the evolutionary history of Ceratopteris based on both genomic and cytogenetic data, and on repeat proportions similar to those found in large flowering plant genomes. This study provides a major stepping-stone in the understanding of land plant evolutionary genomics by providing the first homosporous fern reference genome, as well as insights into the processes underlying the formation of these massive genomes."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Ferns are notorious for possessing large genomes and numerous chromosomes. Despite decades of speculation, the processes underlying the expansive genomes of ferns are unclear, largely due to the absence of a sequenced homosporous fern genome. The lack of this crucial resource has not only hindered investigations of evolutionary processes responsible for the unusual genome characteristics of homosporous ferns, but also impeded synthesis of genome evolution across land plants. Here, we used the model fern species <i>Ceratopteris richardii</i> to address the processes (e.g., polyploidy, spread of repeat elements) by which the large genomes and high chromosome numbers typical of homosporous ferns may have evolved and have been maintained. We directly compared repeat compositions in species spanning the green plant tree of life and a diversity of genome sizes, as well as both short- and long-read-based assemblies of <i>Ceratopteris</i>. We found evidence consistent with a single ancient polyploidy event in the evolutionary history of <i>Ceratopteris</i> based on both genomic and cytogenetic data, and on repeat proportions similar to those found in large flowering plant genomes. This study provides a major stepping-stone in the understanding of land plant evolutionary genomics by providing the first homosporous fern reference genome, as well as insights into the processes underlying the formation of these massive genomes.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>There are estimated to be over 400,000 species of extant land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Lughadha, E. N. et al. Counting counts: Revised estimates of numbers of accepted species of flowering plants, seed plants, vascular plants and land plants with a review of other recent estimates. Phytotaxa 272, 82â88 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d58094e614">1</a></sup>, encompassing an enormous array of morphological, physiological, and ecological diversity. Accompanying this diversity is extraordinary variation in genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bennett, M. D. &amp; Leitch, I. J. Plant DNA C-values database (release 6.0, Dec. 2012). WWW Doc. URL http//data.kew.org/cvalues/. [accessed 14 Oct. 2014] (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d58094e618">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Rice, A. et al. The Chromosome Counts Database (CCDB) â a community resource of plant chromosome numbers. New Phytol. 206, 19â26 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d58094e621">3</a></sup>, spanning a 2,500-fold range from the bladderwort <i>Genlisea aurea</i> (~60 Mbp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Greilhuber, J. et al. Smallest angiosperm genomes found in Lentibulariaceae, with chromosomes of bacterial size. Plant Biol. 8, 770â777 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d58094e628">4</a></sup> to that of the monocot <i>Paris japonica</i> (150 Gbp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pellicer, J., Fay, M. F. &amp; Leitch, I. J. The largest eukaryotic genome of them all? Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 164, 10â15 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d58094e636">5</a></sup>. How these genomes are chromosomally partitioned also varies immensely, as land plants span a 360-fold range in chromosome number, from <i>2n</i>â=â4 in <i>Haplopappus gracilis, Brachychome dichromosomatica, Zingeria biebersteiniana</i>, and <i>Colpodium versicola</i> to <i>2n</i>â=â1,440 in the fern <i>Ophioglossum reticulatum</i>, the highest number reported for any eukaryote<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Rice, A. et al. The Chromosome Counts Database (CCDB) â a community resource of plant chromosome numbers. New Phytol. 206, 19â26 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d58094e656">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ghatak, J. Biosystematic survey of pteridophytes from Shevaroy Hills, south India. Nucleus 20, 105â108 (1977)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d58094e659">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bennett, M. D. Plant genome values: How much do we know? Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 95, 2011â2016 (1998)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d58094e662">7</a></sup>. Understanding the processes underlying this enormous breadth in both genome size and chromosome number has long been a major area of interest among evolutionary biologists. However, sampling biases towards smaller, less complex genomes (e.g., <i>Arabidopsis</i>: 135 Mbp, <i>n</i>â=â5) and crops have pervaded plant genome projects. Fortunately, recent technological advances have enabled the assembly and analysis of large genomes, such as those of conifers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d58094e672">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zimin, A. et al. Sequencing and assembly of the 22-gb loblolly pine genome. Genetics 196, 875â90 (2014)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d58094e672_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Birol, I. et al. Assembling the 20 Gb white spruce (Picea glauca) genome from whole-genome shotgun sequencing data. Bioinforma. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btt178&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d58094e675">10</a></sup>, providing novel insights into the processes underlying genome and chromosomal composition. Despite this progress, the large genomes typical of homosporous ferns remain uninvestigated.</p><p>Polyploidy, or whole-genome duplication (WGD), is the traditional explanation for the large genomes and numerous chromosomes found in many plants, as WGD results in the complete doubling of the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Stebbins, G. L. Jr The significance of polyploidy in plant evolution. Am. Nat. 74, 54â66 (1940)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d58094e682">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Grant, V. Plant Speciation (Columbia University Press, 1981)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d58094e685">12</a></sup>. Among flowering plants, phylogenetic and genomic studies have identified WGD events preceding key radiations, such as those of the core eudicots (~70% of the flowering plants)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jiao, Y. et al. A genome triplication associated with early diversification of the core eudicots. Genome Biol. 13, R3 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d58094e689">13</a></sup>, monocots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J. E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 107, 472â477 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d58094e693">14</a></sup>, and the entirety of flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97â100 (2011)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d58094e697">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella Genome and the Evolution of Flowering Plants. Science (80-.), 342, (2013)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d58094e697_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Landis, J. B. et al. Impact of wholeâgenome duplication events on diversification rates in angiosperms. Am. J. Bot. (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d58094e700">17</a></sup>. In addition, it was demonstrated that even species with minute genomes, such as the carnivorous plant <i>Utricularia gibba</i>, with <i>n</i>â=â13 and a genome size of 80 Mbp, have experienced multiple WGD events; <i>U. gibba</i> has undergone at least three ancient WGD events in the last 80 million years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Carretero-Paulet, L. et al. High Gene Family Turnover Rates and Gene Space Adaptation in the Compact Genome of the Carnivorous Plant Utricularia gibba. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 1284â1295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d58094e714">18</a></sup>. Thus, genome size and chromosome number may not be reliable indicators of WGD. This disparity between genome size and chromosomal composition relative to WGD frequency has altered our understanding of genome evolution as the question has changed from whether or not an organism is polyploid, to how many rounds of polyploidy an organism or lineage has experienced in its evolutionary history.</p><p>Although a few fern genomes, such as those of the heterosporous water ferns (&lt;1% of fern diversity), are less than 250 Mbp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, F.-W. et al. Fern genomes elucidate land plant evolution and cyanobacterial symbioses. Nat. plants, 1 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d58094e721">19</a></sup>, the average homosporous fern genome is 12 Gbp, nearly five times the size of the genome of maize (2.5 Gbp) and over 80 times that of <i>Arabidopsis</i>. In addition, homosporous ferns typically have substantially more chromosomes than seed plants, with an average haploid chromosome number of 59 compared to 16 in flowering plants or 12 in gymnosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Rice, A. et al. The Chromosome Counts Database (CCDB) â a community resource of plant chromosome numbers. New Phytol. 206, 19â26 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d58094e728">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Klekowski, E. &amp; Baker, H. Evolutionary Significance of Polyploidy in the Pteridophyta. Science (80-.). 153, 305â307 (1966)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d58094e731">20</a></sup>. As a result, longstanding hypotheses have proposed that multiple, repeated WGD events were the major factor contributing to the high chromosome numbers and large genomes of ferns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Klekowski, E. &amp; Baker, H. Evolutionary Significance of Polyploidy in the Pteridophyta. Science (80-.). 153, 305â307 (1966)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d58094e735">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Haufler, C. H. Electrophoresis is Modifying Our Concepts of Evolution in Homosporous Pteridophytes. Am. J. Bot. 74, 953â966 (1987)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d58094e735_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wagner, W. H. &amp; Wagner, F. S. Polyploidy in pteridophytes. In Polyploidy 199â214 (Springer, 1980)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d58094e738">22</a></sup>.</p><p>It was originally hypothesized that homosporous ferns undergo intense selection favoring polyploidy to buffer against a putatively high rate of inbreeding that results from their unique life history<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Klekowski, E. &amp; Baker, H. Evolutionary Significance of Polyploidy in the Pteridophyta. Science (80-.). 153, 305â307 (1966)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d58094e745">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Klekowski, E. Genetical features of ferns as contrasted with seed plants. Ann. Missouri Bot. Gard. 59, 138â151 (1972)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d58094e748">23</a></sup>. The homosporous fern life cycle includes a free-living haploid gametophyte phase with the potential for intragametophytic selfing (IGS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Klekowski, E. Genetical features of ferns as contrasted with seed plants. Ann. Missouri Bot. Gard. 59, 138â151 (1972)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d58094e752">23</a></sup>, or gametophytic selfing sensu Haufler <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Haufler, C. H. et al. Sex and the single gametophyte: Revising the homosporous vascular plant life cycle in light of contemporary research. Bioscience 66, 928â937 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d58094e759">24</a></sup> â a process that can produce a completely homozygous diploid plant in a single generation and thus expose any deleterious mutations. However, numerous isozyme analyses demonstrated that fern species with the lowest chromosome numbers within a given genus (ranging from <i>n</i>â=â27 to 52) were functionally diploid, producing typical diploid numbers of isozyme loci rather than multiple loci as seen in truly polyploid species with multiples of these low chromosome numbers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Soltis, D. E. Genetic evidence for diploidy in Equisetum. Am. J. Bot., 908â913 (1986)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d58094e767">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Haufler, C. H. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Evolutionary Significance of Polyploidy in the Pteridophyta., 83, 4389â4393 (1986)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d58094e767_1">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Electrophoretic evidence for genetic diploidy in Psilotum nudum. Am. J. Bot., 1667â1671 (1988)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d58094e770">27</a></sup>. Despite the lack of isozyme evidence for repeated polyploidy in diploid fern species, multiple copies of chlorophyll a/b-binding protein genes were discovered in the diploid fern <i>Polystichum munitum</i>, but the duplicated genes were nonfunctional<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Pichersky, E., Soltis, D. &amp; Soltis, P. Defective chlorophyll a/b-binding protein genes in the genome of a homosporous fern. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 87, 195â199 (1990)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d58094e777">28</a></sup>. Furthermore, early population genetic investigations showed that homosporous ferns have highly variable mating systems and are typically outcrossing, refuting the hypothesized force (intense inbreeding depression via IGS) driving selection for polyploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The distribution of selfing rates in homosporous ferns. Am. J. Bot. (1992)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d58094e781">29</a></sup>. More recently, a genetic linkage map showed that <i>Ceratopteris richardii</i> has one of the highest proportions of duplicated loci among plants (76%) yet lacks large, duplicated blocks that would be indicative of polyploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Nakazato, T., Jung, M.-K., Housworth, E. A., Rieseberg, L. H. &amp; Gastony, G. J. Genetic map-based analysis of genome structure in the homosporous fern Ceratopteris richardii. Genetics 173, 1585â97 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d58094e788">30</a></sup>. In addition, a paralog-age distribution analysis of <i>Ceratopteris</i> estimated an ancient polyploidy event over 180 million years ago (mya); however, the data used for this analysis were from a shallow EST library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Barker, M. S. Evolutionary Genomic Analyses of Ferns Reveal that High Chromosome Numbers are a Product of High Retention and Fewer Rounds of Polyploidy Relative to Angiosperms. Am. Fern J., 99, 136â141 CR-Copyright &amp;#169; 2009 American Fern (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d58094e796">31</a></sup>. Despite little evidence for ancient polyploidy in ferns, chromosome count models suggest that 31% of fern and lycophyte speciation events involve WGD, compared to 15% in flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wood, T. E. et al. The frequency of polyploid speciation in vascular plants. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 106, 13875â13879 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d58094e800">32</a></sup>. However, these estimates of WGD refer to relatively recent polyploidy events (neopolyploidy) evident from chromosome numbers rather than ancient (paleopolyploidy) events deep in evolutionary history.</p><p>Ferns are also the only major lineage of land plants with a significant positive relationship between genome size and chromosome number, suggesting that fern chromosomes are relatively static compared to those of angiosperms and gymnosperms for which no such correlation exists<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Nakazato, T., Barker, M. S., Rieseberg, L. H. &amp; Gastony, G. J. Evolution of the nuclear genome of ferns and lycophytes. In Biology and evolution of ferns and lycophytes (Cambridge University Press, 2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d58094e808">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Sessa, E. B. &amp; Der, J. P. Evolutionary genomics of ferns and lycophytes. In Advances in Botanical Research 78, 215â254 (Elsevier, 2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d58094e811">34</a></sup>. While repeated episodes of WGD followed by extensive silencing and rearrangement cannot be discounted as an explanation for the paradoxical genomic, genetic, and chromosomal composition of ferns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Haufler, C. H. Electrophoresis is Modifying Our Concepts of Evolution in Homosporous Pteridophytes. Am. J. Bot. 74, 953â966 (1987)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d58094e815">21</a></sup>, alternative processes underlying their large genomes and high chromosome numbers must be explored. Most notable among these alternative explanations for the large genomes of ferns is the impact of transposable elements (TEs) on genome size, as TEs make up the majority of genome space in a variety of eukaryotic lineages. For example, TEs are responsible for the difference in genome size between cultivated rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i>, 390 Mbp) and wild rice (<i>O. australiensis</i>, 965 Mbp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Piegu, B. et al. Doubling genome size without polyploidization: Dynamics of retrotransposition-driven genomic expansions in Oryza australiensis, a wild relative of rice. Genome Res. 16, 1262â1269 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d58094e825">35</a></sup>. Phylogenetic reconstructions of major TE families in various plant lineages suggest that bursts of TE insertion result in inflated genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404 LPâ12410 (2004)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d58094e830">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vitte, C. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Analysis of retrotransposon structural diversity uncovers properties and propensities in angiosperm genome evolution. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 103, 17638â17643 (2006)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d58094e830_1">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Estep, M. C., DeBarry, J. D. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. The dynamics of LTR retrotransposon accumulation across 25 million years of panicoid grass evolution. Heredity (Edinb). 110, 194â204 (2013)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d58094e830_2">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bennetzen, J. L. &amp; Wang, H. The contributions of transposable elements to the structure, function, and evolution of plant genomes. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 505â530 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d58094e833">39</a></sup>. However, genome inflation does not seem to be a one-way street, because unequal homologous recombination can eliminate repetitive regions, such as those produced by TEs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genome Size Reduction through Illegitimate Recombination Counteracts Genome Expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075â1079 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d58094e837">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hawkins, J. S., Kim, H., Nason, J. D., Wing, R. A. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Differential lineage-specific amplification of transposable elements is responsible for genome size variation in Gossypium. Genome Res. 16, 1252â1261 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d58094e840">41</a></sup>. Analysis of three conifer âgiga-genomesâ (20â30 Gbp) showed that these large genomes were derived not through WGD, but rather via extensive expansion of ancient TEs (especially retrotransposons) and an apparent inability to shed these repetitive regions via unequal recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d58094e844">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="De La Torre, A. R. et al. Insights into conifer giga-genomes. Plant Physiol. 166, 1724â32 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d58094e847">42</a></sup>. While TEs provide a possible alternative explanation for the large genome sizes of ferns as demonstrated in conifers, they cannot explain the high chromosome numbers of ferns. It is possible that ferns have ancestrally high chromosome numbers and a relatively low rate of WGD, yet this begs the question of how the high chromosome numbers were initially obtained. Aneuploidy or chromosomal fission are also possible explanations for the high chromosomal complement of most ferns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wagner, W. H. &amp; Wagner, F. S. Polyploidy in pteridophytes. In Polyploidy 199â214 (Springer, 1980)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d58094e851">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Haufler, C. H. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Evolutionary Significance of Polyploidy in the Pteridophyta., 83, 4389â4393 (1986)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d58094e854">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Polyploidy and Breeding Systems in Homosporous Pteridophyta: A Reevaluation. Am. Nat. 130, 219â232 (1987)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d58094e857">43</a></sup>.</p><p>There are now hundreds of published flowering plant, gymnosperm, lycophyte, and bryophyte genomes, alongside the recent publication of two heterosporous water fern genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, F.-W. et al. Fern genomes elucidate land plant evolution and cyanobacterial symbioses. Nat. plants, 1 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d58094e864">19</a></sup>. While these water fern genomes, for <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i>, are much-needed references within the fern clade, they are atypical of 99% of ferns, in that these species are heterosporous and have very small genomes with few chromosomes (1âCâ=â0.25â1.76 Gbp, <i>n</i>â=â9â22)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, F.-W. et al. Fern genomes elucidate land plant evolution and cyanobacterial symbioses. Nat. plants, 1 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d58094e877">19</a></sup>. To date, no sequenced genome is yet available for any homosporous fern<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Sessa, E. B. et al. Between Two Fern Genomes. Gigascience, 3, (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d58094e882">44</a></sup>. This major information gap is made more startling when the high species diversity (&gt;10,000 species), significant ecological roles, and economic importance of homosporous ferns are considered<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Durand, L. Z. &amp; Goldstein, G. Photosynthesis, photoinhibition, and nitrogen use efficiency in native and invasive tree ferns in Hawaii. Oecologia 126, 345â354 (2001)." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d58094e886">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ellwood, M. D. F. &amp; Foster, Wa Doubling the estimate of invertebrate biomass in a rainforest canopy. Nature 429, 549â51 (2004)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d58094e886_1">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fayle, T. M., Chung, A. Y. C., Dumbrell, A. J., Eggleton, P. &amp; Foster, W. A. The Effect of Rain Forest Canopy Architecture on the Distribution of Epiphytic Ferns (Asplenium spp.) in Sabah, Malaysia. Biotropica 41, 676â681 (2009)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d58094e886_2">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Paul, B. et al. Azolla domestication towards a biobased economy? New Phytol. 202, 1069â1082 (2014)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d58094e886_3">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Shukla, A. K. et al. Expression of an insecticidal fern protein in cotton protects against whitefly. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 1046 (2016)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d58094e886_4">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="PPG I. A community derived classification for extant lycophytes and ferns. J. Syst. Evol. 54, 563â603 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d58094e889">50</a></sup>. Due to their crucial phylogenetic position as sister to seed plants, ferns are key for investigating an array of both genomic and non-genomic traits and will permit a synthesis of genome evolution across seed plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Pryer, K. M., Schneider, H., Zimmer, E. A. &amp; Ann Banks, J. Deciding among green plants for whole genome studies. Trends Plant Sci. 7, 550â554 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d58094e893">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. A conifer genome spruces up plant phylogenomics. Genome Biol. 14, 122 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d58094e896">52</a></sup>.</p><p>Here we investigated the genome of the homosporous fern <i>Ceratopteris richardii</i> (C-fern; 11.25 Gbp, <i>n</i>â=â39), characterizing and classifying TE composition and assessing the extent of WGD. Our genomic data for C-fern, together with the recently published heterosporous water fern genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, F.-W. et al. Fern genomes elucidate land plant evolution and cyanobacterial symbioses. Nat. plants, 1 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d58094e909">19</a></sup>, help provide a genome evolutionary context not just for ferns, but also for all vascular plants. Collectively, these data will permit deductions about ancestral genome characteristics of seed plants and ferns, as in studies of other phylogenetically pivotal lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella Genome and the Evolution of Flowering Plants. Science (80-.), 342, (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d58094e913">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Warren, W. C. et al. Genome analysis of the platypus reveals unique signatures of evolution. Nature 453, 175â183 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d58094e916">53</a></sup>. Specifically, this <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome provides critical insights into the evolutionary genomics and paradoxes of the genomically long-neglected fern clade, in addition to serving as a valuable reference for future investigations into land plant genome composition and dynamics.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Here we present the first sequence of a homosporous fern genome, providing a new resource for plant and evolutionary biology. The ability of homosporous ferns to undergo IGS (see above) partially simplified the assembly of this complex genome, as it made the sporophyte completely homozygous so that heterozygosity was not an issue in assembly. However, the quality of the <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome assembly and the computational resources required to assemble and analyze the genome reflect the technological difficulties of working with such a large and complicated genome with no closely related reference genome.</p><p>With paired-end short-read libraries totaling ~24X coverage from 1.8 billion cleaned reads, we assembled the 11.25 Gbp <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome into ~15 million contigs (&gt;100âbp) or 988,403 scaffolds (&gt;1,000âbp) (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Tab1">1</a>). We then combined and reduced the number of scaffolds using 8â10Â Kbp mate-pair reads (13X coverage), producing a genome assembly (<i>CFern v1.1</i>) of 626,576 scaffolds with an N50 of 16 Kbp and total length of 4.25 Gbp, representing about 38% of the <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome assembly statistics.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also sampled a smaller portion of the <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome using long-read sequencing of 32 bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) of <i>Ceratopteris</i>. This subsample assembly (<i>BAC.SubSample</i>) only totaled 3 Mbp of the <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome (0.03%), but had an N50 of 97 Kbp, providing a small, but more accurate and contiguous sampling of the 11.25Â Gbp genome as long-read technology is less biased by repeat elements and mis-assemblies. The GC content of <i>Ceratopteris</i> was 37.7%, very similar to that of both the gymnosperm <i>Picea abies</i> (Norway spruce) (37.6%) and the flowering plant <i>Amborella trichocarpa</i> (37.5%), yet lower than that of maize (46.9%), the liverwort <i>Marchantia polymorpha</i> (42.0%), and the lycophyte <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> (45.3%).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Transcriptome assembly</h3><p>From 12 PacBio SMRT cells, we obtained ~850,000 reads from which we produced 97,084 full-length, high-quality, cleaned transcripts (<i>IsoSeq.HQ</i>) ranging from 285 to 11,353âbp in length. When mapped onto the <i>CFern v1.1</i> assembly at 98% identity and 98% coverage, the <i>IsoSeq.HQ</i> transcripts were collapsed into 4,620 genes and 10,043 isoforms; however, when coverage was reduced to 50%, there were 11,924 genes and 23,278 isoforms. The 2.5-fold increase in identified genes and isoforms via reduced coverage shows that our scaffolds do not span entire genes in the majority of cases. To overcome this fragmentation and provide a set of high-confidence gene models, we implemented the Cogent genome-free protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Widespread polycistronic transcripts in fungi revealed by single-molecule mRNA sequencing. PLoS One 10, e0132628 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1166">54</a></sup> to produce 18,179 gene models (<i>UniCFernModels</i>) from the <i>IsoSeq.HQ</i> transcripts. Searching for 1,440 embryophyte single-copy orthologs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1177">55</a></sup>, we found 53% complete, 4.5% fragmented, and 42.6% missing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Polyploidy</h3><p>To address the decades-old question of how common ancient polyploidy is in ferns, we employed sequence-based and cytogenetic approaches, which assessed three different temporal scopes of evolutionary history. Using paralog-age distribution analyses, we identified 1,800 paralogous gene pairs in the <i>UniCFernModels</i> with a <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> value between 0.1 and 2.1. A minor peak around <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub>â=â0.3 was detected; however, such small, ârecentâ peaks are often a result of small-scale gene duplications, not WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Cui, L. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738â749 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1204">56</a></sup>. In contrast, a single major peak was revealed in the synonymous distance plot of <i>Ceratopteris</i>, similar to those observed in <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Equisetum</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig1">1</a>). Based on the significant transition from positive to negative in the SiZER plot, the <i>Ceratopteris</i> peak was at <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub>â=â1.1, compared to 0.8 in <i>Azolla</i> and 0.75 in <i>Equisetum</i>, similar to the original results found by Vanneste <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Vanneste, K., Sterck, L., Myburg, Z., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Mizrachi, E. Horsetails Are Ancient Polyploids: Evidence from Equisetum giganteum. Plant Cell, 1â13 &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1105/tpc.15.00157&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1240">57</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-53968-8/MediaObjects/41598_2019_53968_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-53968-8/MediaObjects/41598_2019_53968_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="681" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Polyploidy analyses of three fern species. (<b>A</b>) Paralog-age distribution analyses and associated SiZER plots of three fern species. Upper panels are <i>Ks</i>-based histograms (0.05 bins) of paralogs in <i>Ceratopteris richardii, Azolla filiculoides</i>, and <i>Equisetum giganteum</i>. Lower panels are SiZER plots of the above paralog-age distribution data and associated smoothing functions where blue indicates significant (Î±â=â0.05) increases, red significant decreases, purple insignificance, and gray too few data points to determine. The white lines show the effective window widths for each bandwidth. Both upper and lower panels are on the same <i>x</i>-axis. (<b>B</b>) MAPS analysis across land plants and the associated WGD events (shown as stars). The percentages of subtrees that contain gene duplications shared by the descendent species of a given node are above the phylogeny (connected by dotted lines). Dates are based on Testo and Sundue<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Testo, W. &amp; Sundue, M. A 4000-species dataset provides new insight into the evolution of ferns. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 105, 200â211 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1269">70</a></sup> and Morris <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 115, E2274âE2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1276">67</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To determine whether the peaks found in these three ferns (<i>Ceratopteris, Azolla, Equisetum</i>) represent a shared WGD rather than three distinct WGD events, we used the Multi-taxon Paleopolyploidy Search (MAPS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, Z. et al. Early genome duplications in conifers and other seed plants. Sci. Adv., 1, (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1288">58</a></sup>. We first recovered 10,182 orthogroups from the clustered amino acid sequences of <i>Ceratopteris, Azolla, Equisetum, Amborella, Selaginella</i>, and <i>Physcomitrella</i>. We isolated 4,836 orthogroups with amino acid sequences from all six species and estimated gene family trees for each orthogroup. Of the subtrees that fit the known fern topology, ((<i>Ceratopteris, Azolla), Equisetum</i>), 34% supported a gene duplication in the most recent common ancestor (MRCA) of <i>Ceratopteris</i> and <i>Azolla</i>, and 19% of subtrees fitting the ((<i>Ceratopteris/Azolla</i>, <i>Equisetum</i>), <i>Amborella</i>) topology supported a gene duplication shared across the three fern species (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig1">1</a>) â relatively low proportions compared to similar studies that identified shared WGD events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, Z. et al. Early genome duplications in conifers and other seed plants. Sci. Adv., 1, (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1321">58</a></sup>. These low proportions suggest three lineage-specific WGD events rather than one or two shared events between the three fern taxa.</p><p>While the previously described methods of data analysis forÂ assessing WGD are appropriate at deeper time scales, both are susceptible to missing more recent WGD events. As mentioned above, relatively recent (close to zero along the <i>x</i>-axis) WGD in the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> plots may be mistakenly attributed to small-scale duplications, while MAPS can only identify WGD events that have occurred prior to the MRCA of the next closest taxon included in the analysis. In the case of <i>Ceratopteris</i>, that would be 280 million years to the divergence of <i>Ceratopteris</i> and <i>Azolla</i>, thus only events older than that can be identified by MAPS.</p><p>Our cytogenetic approach using FISH suggests the ploidy of an organism by localizing 125â150 Kbp BAC DNA fragments to the chromosomes where the DNA fragment is found. If the organism is diploid, only two localizations will be apparent, while a polyploid should have more than two localizations. BAC-FISH evidence of polyploidy is relatively short-lived as studies of <i>Nicotiana</i> allopolyploids found that five million years after the WGD event, the two parental genomes in the polyploid were no longer distinguishable due to genome turnover, mutations, and small-scale duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lim, K. A. R. Y., Matyaksek, R., Kovarik, A. &amp; Leitch, A. R. Genome evolution in allotetraploid. Nicotiana. Biol. J. Linn. Soc. 82, 599â606 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1352">59</a></sup>. However, our BAC-FISH results further corroborated our sequencing results in demonstrating a lack of evidence for recent WGD in <i>Ceratopteris</i>. Significantly, we detected only two primary localizations of each BAC probe we exposed to the <i>Ceratopteris</i> chromosome preparations (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig2">2</a>), suggesting diploidy. In a few cases, weak secondary localizations, or âghost bands,â were found on multiple chromosomes; however, these are likely a result of repeat elements that are distributed throughout the numerous chromosomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-53968-8/MediaObjects/41598_2019_53968_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-53968-8/MediaObjects/41598_2019_53968_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="642" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Fluorescent <i>in situ</i> hybridizations of <i>Ceratopteris</i> chromosome spreads. The fluorescent probes are of 100â150 Kbp DNA fragments from BACs of <i>Ceratopteris</i>. Primary âdiploidâ localizations (red bands labeled with arrows) are shown in all four panels, while weak secondary localizations, most likely reflecting repetitive elements, are apparent in (<b>C</b>); note scattered faint red staining in addition to the two strong primary signals. BACs are from wells A12 (<b>A</b>), B3 (<b>B</b>), A8 (<b>C</b>), and B9 (<b>D</b>) in Plate CR_Ba #624, Green Plant BAC Library Project, Clemson University Genomics Institute.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Repeat diversity</h3><p>In total, ~42% of the <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> assembly was composed of repeat elements (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig3">3a</a>). The <i>Copia</i> LTR RTs were the most prolific with over 800,000 elements making up 16.5% of the assembled genome, followed by the <i>Gypsy</i> LTR RT superfamily with 330,000 elements and accounting for 7.5% of the genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Tab2">2</a>). In comparison, Class II DNA transposons include members of 17 different super-families, yet only totaled 52,000 elements and &lt;1% of the genome. The LINE RTs similarly covered 1.6% of the genome across 64,000 elements. Low-complexity, satellite, and simple repeats all covered &lt;0.5% of the genome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-53968-8/MediaObjects/41598_2019_53968_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-53968-8/MediaObjects/41598_2019_53968_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="384" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Repeat composition, lengths, and insertion timing for representative embryophyte genome assemblies. (<b>A</b>) Genome proportions of repetitive and non-repetitive elements for seven taxa spanning land plants, as well as <i>BAC.SubSample</i>, using genome-based analyses. Genome sizes and N50s for analyzed genome assemblies are also provided. (<b>B</b>) Mean repeat element lengths based on genome assembly analyses (<b>A</b>) for seven embryophyte taxa and <i>BAC.SubSample</i>. (<b>C</b>) Genome proportion of repetitive and non-repetitive elements using read-based clustering analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="NovÃ¡k, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792â793 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1451">111</a></sup>. (<b>D</b>) LTR RT insertion dates in <i>Ceratopteris</i> based on the <i>CFern v1.1A</i> and <i>BAC.SubSample</i> assemblies. Insertion dates were inferred from the similarity of long terminal repeat regions of the LTR RTs and a neutral substitution rate of 6.5âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> per site per year.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 <i>Ceratopteris</i> repeat diversity and composition.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The repeat content and percent coverage were considerably higher in the long-read <i>BAC.SubSample</i> assembly (63%). Nearly 26% of the subsample assembly was made up of <i>Gypsy</i> LTR RTs and 21.8% was <i>Copia</i> LTR RTs, while the LINE RTs and DNA transposons represented 3.2% and 0.16%, respectively. Low-complexity and simple repeats made up 0.2% and 2.2% of the <i>BAC.SubSample</i>, respectively. The mean lengths of all of the repeat types in the <i>BAC.SubSample</i>, with the exception of the DNA TEs, were more than double those of the <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> assembly, and the <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> elements were nearly three times as large in the subsample compared to those of the <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> assembly.</p><p>Similar to the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> results, read-based analyses of <i>Ceratopteris</i> also estimated that ~60% of the <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome is repetitive with 17% in <i>Gypsy</i> elements and 30% in <i>Copia</i> elements (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig3">3c</a>). The read-based analysis and <i>BAC.SubSample</i> characterization analysis are more accurate for assessing general genome composition compared to repeat characterization of the <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> assembly, as they are not biased by the short-read assembly which can have trouble assembling repetitive regions beyond the length of the reads. This assembly bias is probably the reason the <i>CFern v1.1</i> assembly was limited in low-complexity, satellite, and simple repeat elements. This limitation is also apparent in comparing the mean lengths of the <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> LTR RTs to those of the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig3">3b</a>), as the latter assembly could likely span those longer repetitive regions via long-read technology.</p><p>We directly compared the repeat content of <i>Ceratopteris</i> to that of other land plants by applying the same assembly-based repeat characterization protocol to <i>Amborella trichopoda</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella Genome and the Evolution of Flowering Plants. Science (80-.), 342, (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1901">16</a></sup>, a monocot (<i>Zea mays</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Hirsch, C. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell tpc-00353 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1908">60</a></sup>, a liverwort (<i>Marchantia polymorpha</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287â304 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1916">61</a></sup>, a lycophyte (<i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella Genome Identifies Genetic Changes Associated with the Evolution of Vascular. Plants. Sci. 332, 960â963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1923">62</a></sup>, a conifer (<i>Picea abies</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1930">8</a></sup>, and a moss (<i>Physcomitrella patens</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Lang, D. et al. The Physcomitrella patens chromosomeâscale assembly reveals moss genome structure and evolution. Plant J. 93, 515â533 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d58094e1938">63</a></sup>. We chose to run our own analyses on these genome assemblies rather than comparing our results to those of previously published results due to the wide variation in repeat characterization analyses utilized. In addition, we ran the read-based clustering analysis on the above taxa, with the exception of <i>Selaginella</i>, using short reads from these assemblies covering 0.5X of each genome. Due to the previously mentioned limitations of the <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> assembly, we largely focused on the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> and read-based results for comparing relative proportions of repeats to other taxa.</p><p>Compared to six other land plant genome assemblies, the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> was second in repeat proportion behind only <i>Picea</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig3">3a</a>). The <i>BAC.SubSample</i> had proportions of <i>Copia</i> elements similar to those of <i>Zea</i>, substantially higher than any of the other genomes analyzed. The other super-family of LTR RTs, the <i>Gypsy</i> elements, represented 25.7% of the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> with a mean length of 1,660âbp. In contrast, 35% of the <i>Zea</i> and <i>Physcomitrella</i> genomes were made up of <i>Gypsy</i> elements with mean lengths of 2,755 and 1,574âbp, respectively. The read-based analyses generally agreed with the repeat proportions of the six analyzed taxa with the exceptions of <i>Amborella</i> and <i>Picea</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig3">3c</a>). These two taxa had lower overall repeat proportions in the read-based analyses (58% vs. 25% in <i>Amborella</i>, 80% vs. 50% in <i>Picea</i>), matching previous similar studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Wolf, P. G. et al. An exploration into fern genome space. Genome Biol. Evol. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/gbe/evv163&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2008">64</a></sup>.</p><p>We assessed LTR RT richness by comparing recent (&gt;90% LTR similarity) LTR RT exemplars among the seven species compared here (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Tab3">3</a>). <i>Zea</i> was by far the most diverse with 4,561 distinct LTR RT exemplars, followed by <i>Physcomitrella</i> at 1,217 exemplars and <i>Picea</i> with 509. The <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> assembly was low in recent LTR RT diversity with only 22 exemplars, similar to that of <i>Amborella</i> and <i>Marchantia</i>, which had 11 and 30, respectively. Ancient (75â90% LTR similarity) LTR RT richness differed greatly from recent LTR RT diversity in <i>Zea</i> and <i>Physcomitrella</i>, which only had 45 and 16 ancient exemplars, respectively (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Tab3">3</a>). <i>Picea</i> also had fewer ancient exemplars than recent LTR RTs with 276, but <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> and <i>Amborella</i> both had more ancient than recent LTR RT exemplars with 82 and 55, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Genome composition and LTR-RT statistics in sampled land plant genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The quality of the genome assembly could have had a large effect on these interspecific comparisons of repeat diversity, number, and size, as well as genome size, as earlier demonstrated with the <i>BAC.SubSample</i>. For example, these eight genome assemblies spanned a 27,000-fold difference in scaffold N50 lengths between that of <i>Picea</i> (8 Kbp) and maize (217,960 Kbp). Thus, in addition to the genomes of <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> and <i>Picea</i> being many times larger than that of maize, they are much more fragmented, making the identification of repeat elements more difficult and biased for identifying those repeats with smaller lengths.</p><p>To investigate LTR RT insertion timing, we identified 62 full-length, high-confidence LTR RT elements in the <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> and <i>BAC.SubSample</i> assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: A Highly Accurate and Sensitive Program for Identification of Long Terminal Repeat Retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410â1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2223">65</a></sup>. The insertion timing of these LTR RTs was relatively uniform over the past 7 million years (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig3">3d</a>). However, we found considerable differences in the LTR RT identification of these two assemblies as the majority of the identified LTR RTs in the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> originated within the last million years, while the <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> assembly did not identify a single LTR RT within the past million years and instead had largely older (&gt;4 mya) LTR RTs. In addition, we note that the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> had 28 full-length, high-confidence LTR RTs, while the <i>CFern v1.1</i>â<i>A</i> assembly had 34, despite nearly a 1,000-fold difference in assembly length. These results suggest that the long-read sequencing of the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> was able to span and properly assemble these repetitive elements, while the short reads of <i>CFern v1.1</i> could only assemble older, more heterogeneous repetitive elements.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Ferns are the second most species-rich clade of vascular plants, with over 10,000 species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="PPG I. A community derived classification for extant lycophytes and ferns. J. Syst. Evol. 54, 563â603 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2265">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Smith, A. R. et al. A classification for extant ferns. Taxon 55, 705â731 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2268">66</a></sup>. In addition, ferns are the sister lineage to all seed plants and first appeared approximately 430 million years ago according to fossil-calibrated phylogenies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 115, E2274âE2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2272">67</a></sup>. The oldest unequivocal fossil fern is <i>Ibyka amphikoma</i> with a minimum age of 384 million years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 115, E2274âE2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2279">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Skog, J. E. &amp; Banks, H. P. Ibyka amphikoma, gen. et sp. n., a new protoarticulate precursor from the late Middle Devonian of New York State. Am. J. Bot. 60, 366â380 (1973)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2282">68</a></sup>. However, most extant fern diversity arose within the last 40â60 million years during the Cenozoic Era following the rise to dominance of the angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Schuettpelz, E. &amp; Pryer, K. M. Fern phylogeny inferred from 400 leptosporangiate species and three plastid genes. Taxon 56, 1037 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2286">69</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Testo, W. &amp; Sundue, M. A 4000-species dataset provides new insight into the evolution of ferns. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 105, 200â211 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2289">70</a></sup>. Despite their substantial morphological diversity, sister relationship to seed plants, and lengthy evolutionary history, ferns represent the final frontier of land plant genomics.</p><p>Here we provide the first draft genome assembly of the 11.25Â Gbp <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome, as well as a high-confidence set of gene models. We assessed the role of WGD in the evolutionary history of <i>Ceratopteris</i> at three distinct temporal scales. Despite a genome size five times that of classically âlarge-genomeâ flowering plants (e.g., maize) and with eight times more chromosomes than <i>Arabidopsis</i>, which has undergone at least five WGD events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella Genome and the Evolution of Flowering Plants. Science (80-.), 342, (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2305">16</a></sup>, we found evidence consistent with onlyÂ one ancient WGD event in <i>Ceratopteris</i>. The diploid signal localizations of our BAC-FISH approach refute any recent WGD events that may have been ambiguous in the paralog-age distribution analyses. The three peaks in the paralog-age distribution analyses of <i>Ceratopteris, Azolla</i>, and <i>Equisetum</i> overlap and thus could potentially be a shared event before the divergence of these three ferns (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig1">1</a>). However, MAPS analysis indicates that only a minority of subtrees support shared duplications among these three taxa, suggesting three lineage-specific WGD events rather than a single shared event. Based on our analyses and the timing of the WGD event in <i>Equisetum</i>, another lineage belonging to the broader fern (monilophyte) clade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Vanneste, K., Sterck, L., Myburg, Z., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Mizrachi, E. Horsetails Are Ancient Polyploids: Evidence from Equisetum giganteum. Plant Cell, 1â13 &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1105/tpc.15.00157&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2325">57</a></sup>, the WGD of <i>Ceratopteris</i> is likely older than that of <i>Equisetum</i> (92 mya) yet younger than the most recent common ancestor of <i>Ceratopteris</i> and <i>Azolla</i> (~280 mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Testo, W. &amp; Sundue, M. A 4000-species dataset provides new insight into the evolution of ferns. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 105, 200â211 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2342">70</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig1">1b</a>). The approaches used here are those standardly used for inferring ancient WGD events from transcriptomic and genomic data. Even complete transcriptomes or chromosome-level genomes can underestimate the true number of WGDs as pseudogenes and gene fragments may be filtered out during analyses. As such, all paralog-age distribution analyses and the resulting inferences regarding WGDs are considered minimum estimates; however, similar analyses of diploid and triploid <i>Ceratopteris thalictroides</i> found nearly identical <i>Ks</i> frequency distributions to the results presented in this study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Zhang, R. et al. Dating Whole Genome Duplication in Ceratopteris thalictroides and Potential Adaptive Values of Retained Gene Duplicates. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 20, 1926 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2356">71</a></sup>. If additional WGD events were obscured from the <i>Ks</i> analyses due to incomplete gene sampling, it is unlikely that transcriptome samples from a congeneric species would hide the same events. The authors of the above-mentioned study dated the WGD event to 52 Â± 1 mya due to their use of a relatively rapid synonymous substitution rate (11.04âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Zhang, R. et al. Dating Whole Genome Duplication in Ceratopteris thalictroides and Potential Adaptive Values of Retained Gene Duplicates. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 20, 1926 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2365">71</a></sup>. Synonymous substitution rates are highly variable among plant lineages and across time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="De La Torre, A. R., Li, Z., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Ingvarsson, P. K. Contrasting rates of molecular evolution and patterns of selection among gymnosperms and flowering plants. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1363â1377 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2369">72</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Bromham, L., Hua, X., Lanfear, R. &amp; Cowman, P. F. Exploring the relationships between mutation rates, life history, genome size, environment, and species richness in flowering plants. Am.
