Nat. 185, 507â524 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2372">73</a></sup>; thus, we are more confident in our relative dating of the <i>Ceratopteris</i> WGD as occurring between that of <i>Equisetum</i> and the most recent common ancestor of <i>Ceratopteris</i> and <i>Azolla</i> due to our MAPS results.</p><p>Our results do not support hypotheses of frequent WGD in ferns followed by massive gene silencing and the slow loss of genetic material<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Haufler, C. H. Homospory 2002: An Odyssey of progress in pteridophyte genetics and evolutionary biology: Ferns and other homosporous vascular plants have highly polyploid chromosome numbers, but they express traits following diploid models and, although capable of extre. AIBS Bull. 52, 1081â1093 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2392">74</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Haufler, C. H. Ever since Klekowski: testing a set of radical hypotheses revives the genetics of ferns and lycophytes. Am. J. Bot. 101, 2036â2042 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2395">75</a></sup> and instead lend credence to the hypothesis that ferns had ancestrally high chromosome numbers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Haufler, C. H. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Evolutionary Significance of Polyploidy in the Pteridophyta., 83, 4389â4393 (1986)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2399">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Polyploidy and Breeding Systems in Homosporous Pteridophyta: A Reevaluation. Am. Nat. 130, 219â232 (1987)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2402">43</a></sup> and underwent WGD rarely yet were unable or very slow to lose genetic material<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Haufler, C. H. Electrophoresis is Modifying Our Concepts of Evolution in Homosporous Pteridophytes. Am. J. Bot. 74, 953â966 (1987)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2406">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Barker, M. S. Evolutionary Genomic Analyses of Ferns Reveal that High Chromosome Numbers are a Product of High Retention and Fewer Rounds of Polyploidy Relative to Angiosperms. Am. Fern J., 99, 136â141 CR-Copyright &amp;#169; 2009 American Fern (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2409">31</a></sup>. This conclusion is in agreement with past studies based on isozymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Soltis, D. E. Genetic evidence for diploidy in Equisetum. Am. J. Bot., 908â913 (1986)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2413">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Haufler, C. H. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Evolutionary Significance of Polyploidy in the Pteridophyta., 83, 4389â4393 (1986)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2413_1">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Electrophoretic evidence for genetic diploidy in Psilotum nudum. Am. J. Bot., 1667â1671 (1988)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2416">27</a></sup>, transcripts of <i>Equisetum giganteum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Vanneste, K., Sterck, L., Myburg, Z., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Mizrachi, E. Horsetails Are Ancient Polyploids: Evidence from Equisetum giganteum. Plant Cell, 1â13 &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1105/tpc.15.00157&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2422">57</a></sup>, a genetic linkage map of <i>Ceratopteris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Nakazato, T., Jung, M.-K., Housworth, E. A., Rieseberg, L. H. &amp; Gastony, G. J. Genetic map-based analysis of genome structure in the homosporous fern Ceratopteris richardii. Genetics 173, 1585â97 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2429">30</a></sup>, as well as ancestral reconstructions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Clark, J. et al. Genome evolution of ferns: evidence for relative stasis of genome size across the fern phylogeny. New Phytol. 210, 1072â1082 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2433">76</a></sup> that found ancient WGD events to be rare in the evolutionary history of ferns despite the presence of many neopolyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wood, T. E. et al. The frequency of polyploid speciation in vascular plants. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 106, 13875â13879 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2437">32</a></sup>. While genomic analyses in flowering plants have shown that even very small genomes, such as that of <i>Arabidopsis</i>, have undergone numerous rounds of polyploidy, yet still have a low number of chromosomes, we find that ferns are much less dynamic, having undergone relatively few WGD events, yet retaining a high number of chromosomes. Ancestral reconstructions of chromosome numbers across ferns have suggested that the common ancestor of all ferns had a haploid chromosome number of 22, while many of the more diverse fern lineages had higher ancestral chromosome numbers, such as <i>n</i>â=â30 in Pteridaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Clark, J. et al. Genome evolution of ferns: evidence for relative stasis of genome size across the fern phylogeny. New Phytol. 210, 1072â1082 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2448">76</a></sup>. If chromosome numbers were ancestrally high in ferns, only a single WGD event would therefore be needed to reach a chromosome number of <i>n</i>â=â39 in <i>Ceratopteris</i> (or <i>n</i>â=â59, the average across all ferns) since the divergence of the common ancestor of ferns from that of seed plants 400 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 115, E2274âE2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2461">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Testo, W. &amp; Sundue, M. A 4000-species dataset provides new insight into the evolution of ferns. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 105, 200â211 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2464">70</a></sup>. Alternatively, the high chromosome numbers of ferns could be a result of aneuploidy or chromosomal fission<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="De La Torre, A. R. et al. Insights into conifer giga-genomes. Plant Physiol. 166, 1724â32 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2468">42</a></sup>. To better understand the cause of the high chromosome numbers of homosporous ferns, comparative syntenic and phylogenomic analyses will have to be applied across multiple fern taxa based on complete genome assemblies.</p><p>Similar to other large plant genomes, a large proportion of the <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome is composed of LTR RTs and other transposable elements (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#Fig3">3</a>). This âgenome obesityâ is the likely result of a steady accumulation of transposable elements and an inability to discard them, as found in smaller flowering plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2481">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genome Size Reduction through Illegitimate Recombination Counteracts Genome Expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075â1079 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2484">40</a></sup>. Importantly, <i>Ceratopteris</i> had a very low diversity of recent LTR RT exemplars when compared to other large-genome species such as maize or <i>Picea</i>. While this finding could be indicative of low LTR RT richness and high abundance, given that the counts of the LTR RTs were considerably higher in <i>Ceratopteris</i> compared to the other genomes, it is also possible that we are unable to identify the majority of full-length LTR RTs due to low scaffold contiguity with the <i>CFern v1.1</i> assembly. The <i>BAC.SubSample</i> assembly and read-based analyses provide a more accurate representation of the general repeat composition of <i>Ceratopteris</i>; however, these results provide a much smaller representation of the genome and are limited in their resolution. Clearly, long-read technology will be necessary to overcome and fully analyze a genome of this size, as short-read sequencing simply cannot span and assemble the repetitive structures found in <i>Ceratopteris</i>. However, expense must be taken into account in any sequencing project, and here the deep sequencing of an 11.25Â Gbp genome using long-read technology would take much longer and cost much more than similar sequencing with short reads.</p><p>This study provides a major stepping-stone in the understanding of plant evolutionary genomics by providing the first homosporous fern reference genome, as well as unique insights into the processes underlying the formation of these massive genomes. Future efforts should focus on long-read technology to provide a complete assembly of multiple homosporous fernsâthus permitting more extensive comparisons of genome evolution and synteny across green plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Tissue samples</h3><p><i>Ceratopteris richardii</i> (Pteridaceae) is a fast-growing tropical fern, used globally in research laboratories as well as in K-12 and undergraduate biology courses for studying alternation of generations in plants. Inbred lines and single-gene mutants are commercially available and readily produced. For this study, spores from the Hn-n inbred line were kindly donated by Dr. Leslie Hickok (University of Tennessee). The spores were germinated on nutrient media<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Bold, H. C. Morphology of Plants (Harper and Row, 1957)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2528">77</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Nitsch, J. P. Growth and development in vitro of excised ovaries. Am. J. Bot., 566â577 (1951)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2531">78</a></sup> and grown following the recommended conditions in the C-Fern Manual (<a href="http://www.c-fern.org">www.c-fern.org</a>). We isolated the germinated gametophytes to individual petri dishes and growth media. Given that <i>C. richardii</i> is homosporous, the gametophytes are typically bisexual and produce both antheridia and archegonia. By isolating the gametophytes prior to sexual maturity, we ensured that any sporophytes that did develop were a product of gametes from a single gametophyte and thus completely homozygous (doubled haploid).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Library construction and sequencing</h3><p>We extracted genomic DNA (gDNA) from <i>Ceratopteris</i> using a modified CTAB protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Doyle, J. &amp; Doyle, J. L. Genomic plant DNA preparation from fresh tissue-CTAB method. Phytochem Bull 19, 11â15 (1987)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2553">79</a></sup> and quality checked and quantified the gDNA using a Qubit fluorometer (Invitrogen, Carlsbad, CA, USA) and NanoDrop spectrophotometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific, Waltham, MA, USA). Genomic short-read library preparation and sequencing for <i>Ceratopteris</i> were completed by the University of Floridaâs Interdisciplinary Center for Biotechnology Research (UF ICBR). The gDNA was fragmented and size-selected for ~300 base pair (bp) inserts, and the sequencing of 150Â bp paired-end (PE) reads was conducted on two runs of the Illumina NextSeq platform (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA). Mate-pair (MP) libraries (125âbp PE, 8â10 Kbp inserts) were prepared and sequenced at the Duke Genome Sequencing and Analysis Core on two lanes of Illumina HiSeq 2000 (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA).</p><p>We also subsampled the <i>Ceratopteris</i> genome with long-read technology to avoid the assembly biases inherent in short-read technology by sequencing BAC clones (Plate CR_Ba #624, Green Plant BAC Library Project, provided by Clemson University Genomics Institute). We selected 34 <i>Ceratopteris</i> Hn-n BAC clones to be grown, pooled, purified, and sequenced using the RS II platform (Pacific Bioscience, Menlo Park, CA, USA) at the Arizona Genomics Institute. The reads were cleaned and <i>de novo</i> assembled using the Hierarchical Genome Assembly Process (HGAP) in the SMRT Analysis software package (Pacific Biosciences, Menlo Park, CA, USA) to produce the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> assembly.</p><p>Long-read technology was also used to acquire a high-confidence set of gene models from sporophyte tissue. We extracted total RNA from sexually mature leaf tissue using the RNeasy Plant Mini kit (Qiagen, Hilden, Germany). The total RNA was size-selected for 0.8â2, 2â3, 3â5, and &gt;5 Kbp with the SageELF (Sage Science, Beverly, MA, USA) at the UF ICBR. The libraries were prepared following the SMRTbell library protocol, and each library was sequenced on three PacBio SMRT cells (Pacific Bioscience, Menlo Park, CA, USA) at the UF ICBR.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly</h3><p>The raw genomic PE reads were trimmed of adapters and then quality-filtered with Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2586">80</a></sup>, while the raw MP reads were trimmed of adapters and separated into MP, PE, and unknown reads with NxTrim<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="OâConnell, J. et al. NxTrim: optimized trimming of Illumina mate pair reads. Bioinformatics 31, 2035â2037 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2590">81</a></sup>. All libraries were quality-checked before and after cleaning with FastQC (<a href="http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/">www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/</a>). We divided the cleaned PE reads into 24-mers with Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="MarÃ§ais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2598">82</a></sup> and plotted their frequencies with KAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Mapleson, D., Garcia Accinelli, G., Kettleborough, G., Wright, J. &amp; Clavijo, B. J. KAT: a K-mer analysis toolkit to quality control NGS datasets and genome assemblies. Bioinformatics 33, 574â576 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2602">83</a></sup> to assess environmental contamination, organellar genome content, nuclear genome size, and repeat content.</p><p>The PE reads were assembled using Meraculous2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Chapman, J. A., Ho, I. Y., Goltsman, E. &amp; Rokhsar, D. S. Meraculous2: fast accurate short-read assembly of large polymorphic genomes. arXiv Prepr. arXiv1608.01031 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2609">84</a></sup> and a k-mer size of 61 based on the results of KmerGenie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Chikhi, R. &amp; Medvedev, P. Informed and automated k-mer size selection for genome assembly. Bioinformatics 30, 31â37 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2613">85</a></sup> to produce assembly <i>CFern v1.0</i>. The scaffolds from the <i>CFern v1.0</i> assembly were further scaffolded with the MP reads using the SSPACE assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578â579 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2623">86</a></sup> to produce the genome assembly <i>CFern v1.1</i>. To compare the content of <i>CFern v1.1</i> with the overall content of the cleaned reads, we divided the assembly into 24-mers with Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="MarÃ§ais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2634">82</a></sup> and compared the resulting frequencies to those of the cleaned PE reads using the compare feature of KAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Mapleson, D., Garcia Accinelli, G., Kettleborough, G., Wright, J. &amp; Clavijo, B. J. KAT: a K-mer analysis toolkit to quality control NGS datasets and genome assemblies. Bioinformatics 33, 574â576 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2638">83</a></sup>. For subsequent analyses, only scaffolds over 10 Kbp were used (<i>CFern v1.1A</i>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Transcriptome assembly</h3><p>We cleaned and processed the long reads following the IsoSeq protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Widespread polycistronic transcripts in fungi revealed by single-molecule mRNA sequencing. PLoS One 10, e0132628 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2653">54</a></sup> in which the circular consensus sequences (CCS) were acquired from the raw reads and then classified and clustered. Only full-length, high-quality (accuracyâ&gt;=â99%), polished sequences (<i>IsoSeq.HQ</i>) were used for analysis following the Iterative Clustering and Error correction (ICE)/Quiver algorithm. The <i>IsoSeq.HQ</i> sequences were further collapsed into unique isoforms and genes using both genome-based and sequence-based protocols (see below).</p><p>For the genome-based method, the <i>IsoSeq.HQ</i> sequences were mapped to the <i>CFern v1.1A</i> assembly using GMAP (parameters: -f samse ân 0 âz sense_force)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859â1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2672">87</a></sup>. The sam file output was sorted (parameters: -k 3,3 âk 4,4n), and transcripts were collapsed together (collapse_isoforms_by_sam.py, <a href="https://github.com/Magdoll/cDNA_Cupcake">https://github.com/Magdoll/cDNA_Cupcake</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Widespread polycistronic transcripts in fungi revealed by single-molecule mRNA sequencing. PLoS One 10, e0132628 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2680">54</a></sup>. We used both 98% coverage and 98% identity as our full-length mapping cutoff and then searched for incomplete genes with 50% coverage and 98% identity.</p><p>Due to the fragmented state of the <i>CFern v1.1A</i> assembly, many transcripts did not map. Thus, we also used CD-Hit v4.6.4 (parameters: -c 0.99 âG 0 âaL 0.90 âAL 100 âaS 0.99 âAS 30)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Fu, L., Niu, B., Zhu, Z., Wu, S. &amp; Li, W. CD-HIT: accelerated for clustering the next-generation sequencing data. Bioinformatics 28, 3150â3152 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2690">88</a></sup> to cluster and collapse highly similar transcripts into putative isoforms without a reference genome. We then used those sequences with the Coding Genome reconstruction tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Workman, R. E. et al. Single-molecule, full-length transcript sequencing provides insight into the extreme metabolism of the ruby-throated hummingbird Archilochus colubris. Gigascience 7, giy009 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2694">89</a></sup> for genome-free isoform collapse and gene identification. This pipeline divided the sequences into 30-mers and then grouped those kmers into clusters based on pairwise distances. De Bruijn graphs of the sequences for each cluster were then used to resolve sequencing errors and alternative splicing events and output putative genes. Due to the high accuracy, full-length, <i>de novo</i> nature of IsoSeq and subsequent cleaning protocols, these genes served as our reference gene models for <i>Ceratopteris</i> (referred to as <i>UniCFernModels</i>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Polyploidy</h3><p>The <i>UniCFernModels</i> data set was used in the DupPipe pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Barker, M. S. et al. Multiple paleopolyploidizations during the evolution of the Compositae reveal parallel patterns of duplicate gene retention after millions of years. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 2445â2455 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2720">90</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Barker, M. S. et al. EvoPipes. net: bioinformatic tools for ecological and evolutionary genomics. Evol. Bioinforma. 6, EBOâS5861 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2723">91</a></sup> to estimate the relative age of gene duplications. DupPipe finds duplicate gene pairs and then estimates the divergence of these genes using the number of substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub>). The frequency of duplicate genes corresponding to a given level of divergence, as a substitute for timing, was plotted as a histogram, and peaks were inferred to represent synchronous gene duplications, indicative of ancient polyploidy events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Barker, M. S. et al. Multiple paleopolyploidizations during the evolution of the Compositae reveal parallel patterns of duplicate gene retention after millions of years. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 2445â2455 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2733">90</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The Evolutionary Fate and Consequences of Duplicate. Genes. Sci. 290, 1151â1155 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2736">92</a></sup>. Genes from two other ferns, <i>Equisetum giganteum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Vanneste, K., Sterck, L., Myburg, Z., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Mizrachi, E. Horsetails Are Ancient Polyploids: Evidence from Equisetum giganteum. Plant Cell, 1â13 &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1105/tpc.15.00157&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2743">57</a></sup> and <i>Azolla filiculoides</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, F.-W. et al. Fern genomes elucidate land plant evolution and cyanobacterial symbioses. Nat. plants, 1 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2749">19</a></sup>, were similarly analyzed and plotted for comparison. To reduce the subjectivity of smoothing based on varying bin sizes, we analyzed the <i>K</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> values of these three ferns using the SiZer (Significance of Zero Crossings of the Derivative)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Chaudhuri, P. &amp; Marron, J. S. SiZer for exploration of structures in curves. J. Am. Stat. Assoc. 94, 807â823 (1999)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2759">93</a></sup> package in R v3.4.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="R Core Team. R: A language and environment for statistical computing. (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2763">94</a></sup>. This analysis determines whether an increase or decrease in a scatterplot or histogram is significant at Î±â=â0.05 and plots the changes along the original <i>x</i>-axis with blue coloration indicating a significant increase, red a significant decrease, purple insignificance, and gray too few data points to determine.</p><p>To determine whether the three ferns examined here (<i>Ceratopteris</i>, <i>Equisetum</i>, and <i>Azolla</i>), spanning over 400 million years since their most recent common ancestor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Testo, W. &amp; Sundue, M. A 4000-species dataset provides new insight into the evolution of ferns. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 105, 200â211 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2783">70</a></sup>, share any ancient polyploidy events, we clustered the predicted proteins of <i>Ceratopteris, Equisetum, Azolla, Amborella, Selaginella</i>, and <i>Physcomitrella</i> into orthogroups using OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2794">95</a></sup>. Only orthogroups with gene representatives from all six species were retained. The protein sequences of each orthogroup were aligned with MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772â780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2798">96</a></sup>, and the alignments were converted to nucleotide alignments using the pxaa2cdn tool in Phyx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Brown, J. W., Walker, J. F. &amp; Smith, S. A. Phyx: phylogenetic tools for unix. Bioinformatics 33, 1886â1888 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2802">97</a></sup>. The alignments were stripped of highly ambiguous (&gt;90% missing data) columns, and gene trees were produced with RAxML using 100 rapid bootstrap searches and the GTRGAMMA model of evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinforma. 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2806">98</a></sup>. These gene family trees were entered into the Multi-tAxon Paleopolyploidy Search (MAPS) package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, Z. et al. Early genome duplications in conifers and other seed plants. Sci. Adv., 1, (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2810">58</a></sup>. This package first filters all of the gene family trees for subtrees that match the known species tree [here (<i>Physcomitrella</i>, (<i>Selaginella</i>, (<i>Amborella</i>, (<i>Equisetum</i>, (<i>Ceratopteris</i>, <i>Azolla</i>)))))]. It then counts the number of subtrees with gene duplications at a specific node in the species tree relative to the number of available subtrees. A node with a high proportion of gene duplications is presumed to have a shared polyploidy event.</p><p>We also used a cytogenetic approach to assess more recent WGD. We conducted fluorescent <i>in situ</i> hybridization (FISH) using the previously described BAC clones as probes following Chester <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Chester, M. et al. Extensive chromosomal variation in a recently formed natural allopolyploid species, Tragopogon miscellus (Asteraceae). Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 109, 1176â1181 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2842">99</a></sup> and Chamala <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Chamala, S. et al. Assembly and validation of the genome of the nonmodel basal angiosperm Amborella. Science 342, 1516â7 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2849">100</a></sup>. To produce the probes, the BAC DNA was extracted from the <i>Escherichia coli</i> culture and amplified by rolling circle amplification (RCA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Berr, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Direct labelling of BAC DNA by rolling circle amplification. Plant J. 45, 857â862 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2857">101</a></sup>. The RCA product was labeled by nick translation with Cy5-dUTP and purified with a QIAquick Nucleotide Removal kit (Qiagen, Venlo, Netherlands).</p><p>Root tips for chromosome preparations were collected in the mornings and immediately treated with pressurized nitrous oxide for 1âhour before being fixed in 3:1 ethanol (EtOH): glacial acetic acid overnight at room temperature and transferred to 70% EtOH at â20âÂ°C for long-term storage. The root tips were then treated and chromosome spreads prepared to produce slides for <i>in situ</i> hybridization with the fluorescently labeled probes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Chester, M. et al. Extensive chromosomal variation in a recently formed natural allopolyploid species, Tragopogon miscellus (Asteraceae). Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 109, 1176â1181 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2867">99</a></sup>. The BAC FISH images were taken on an AxioImager M2 microscope with an AxioCam MR camera (Carl Zeiss AG, Oberkochen, Germany).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Repeat characterization</h3><p>We took both structural- and homology-based approaches to repeat characterization following Campbell <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513â524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2882">102</a></sup>. As long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR RTs) comprise a sizable proportion of most plant genomes, a variety of tools was used to characterize these repeats in the <i>CFern v1.1A</i> assembly. Recent LTR RTs were collected based on 90% LTR similarity using LTRharvest (parameters: -minlenltr 100 -maxlenltr 6000 -mindistltr 1500 -maxdistltr 25000 -mintsd 5 -maxtsd 5 -motif tgca -similar 90 -vic 10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2889">103</a></sup> from the GenomeTools package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Gremme, G., Steinbiss, S. &amp; Kurtz, S. GenomeTools: a comprehensive software library for efficient processing of structured genome annotations. IEEE/ACM Trans. Comput. Biol. Bioinforma. 10, 645â656 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2893">104</a></sup>. LTRdigest was then used to find elements with poly purine tracts (PPT) or primer binding sites (PBS) using the Genomic tRNA database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Chan, P. P. &amp; Lowe, T. M. GtRNAdb 2.0: an expanded database of transfer RNA genes identified in complete and draft genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D184âD189 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2898">105</a></sup>. Those elements were identified and further filtered for false positive elements by removing gappy elements (&gt;50 Ns), recent gene duplications where the flanking regions of the LTRs are alignable, and nested RT insertions using custom scripts. LTR RTs with nested DNA transposons were also identified by searching DNA transposase protein sequences with BLASTx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2902">106</a></sup>. LTR RT exemplars were then identified based on 80% identity and 90% coverage from the filtered elements based on the internal sequences of the LTR RTs and then based on the LTR sequences. Older LTR RTs were similarly collected but with 75% similarity among the LTR sequences and lacking the TGCA motif. To exclude more recent LTR RTs from the older LTR RT library, the younger LTR RT exemplars were used to mask and exclude elements found in the older LTR RT library with RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Smith, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. 2013â2015." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2906">107</a></sup>. The two LTR RT libraries were combined (<i>allLTR.lib</i>) and used as the reference library to mask the <i>CFern v1.1A</i> assembly with RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Smith, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. 2013â2015." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2916">107</a></sup>.</p><p>The unmasked remainder of the assembly was inputted into RepeatModeler to identify repeat families <i>de novo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Smit, A. F. A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0. Available fom http//www.repeatmasker.org (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2925">108</a></sup>. The RepeatModeler library and LTR RT library were combined, and unidentified repeats were searched against a transposase database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Smith, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. 2013â2015." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2929">107</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Kennedy, R. C., Unger, M. F., Christley, S., Collins, F. H. &amp; Madey, G. R. An automated homology-based approach for identifying transposable elements. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 130 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2932">109</a></sup> using BLASTx and identified to superfamily when possible<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2936">106</a></sup>. To ensure that fragmented plant genes were not included in the final repeat library, we queried all of our repeats with the SwissProt plant protein<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Schneider, M. et al. The UniProtKB/Swiss-Prot knowledgebase and its Plant Proteome Annotation Program. J. Proteomics 72, 567â573 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2940">110</a></sup> and NCBI RefSeq plant protein databases using BLASTx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2944">106</a></sup>. With our clean, final repeat library, we used RepeatMasker to quantify the repeat elements throughout <i>CFern v1.1A</i>.</p><p>To make direct comparisons with other plant genome assemblies of varying sizes, qualities, and lineages, we followed the same repeat annotation protocol for the genomes of <i>Amborella trichopoda</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella Genome and the Evolution of Flowering Plants. Science (80-.), 342, (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2957">16</a></sup>, a monocot (<i>Zea mays</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Hirsch, C. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell tpc-00353 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2964">60</a></sup>, a liverwort (<i>Marchantia polymorpha</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287â304 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2971">61</a></sup>, a lycophyte (<i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella Genome Identifies Genetic Changes Associated with the Evolution of Vascular. Plants. Sci. 332, 960â963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2979">62</a></sup>, a conifer (<i>Picea abies</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2986">8</a></sup>, and a moss (<i>Physcomitrella patens</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Lang, D. et al. The Physcomitrella patens chromosomeâscale assembly reveals moss genome structure and evolution. Plant J. 93, 515â533 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d58094e2993">63</a></sup>. We also ran the same protocol on the <i>BAC.SubSample</i> assembly. To remove assembly biases, we used RepeatExplorer 2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="NovÃ¡k, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792â793 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d58094e3001">111</a></sup> on cleaned reads for the above-mentioned taxa, with the exception of <i>Selaginella</i> which only used Sanger sequencing. Raw reads were downloaded from the NCBI Sequence Read Archive and EMBL European Nucleotide Archive, cleaned and reduced to 0.5X coverage of their respective genomes, then run in RepeatExplorer2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="NovÃ¡k, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792â793 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d58094e3008">111</a></sup> via the Elixir CZ Galaxy portal under default parameters against the Viridiplantae version 3.0 transposable element protein domain database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Dating repeat insertion events</h3><p>We used the highly accurate but conservative LTR_Retriever package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: A Highly Accurate and Sensitive Program for Identification of Long Terminal Repeat Retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410â1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d58094e3020">65</a></sup> to identify full-length LTR RTs and date their insertion using both the <i>CFern v1.1A</i> and <i>BAC.SubSample</i> assemblies. We provided candidate LTR RTs from LTR_harvest and LTR_finder using a 90% similarity minimum threshold between LTRs and the presence of the TGCA motifs. The candidate LTR RTs were filtered, removing non-LTR RT repeat elements or those with large amounts of tandem repeats or gaps. Especially in fragmented genome assemblies, such as the <i>CFern v1.1A</i>, these requirements hugely reduce the number of LTR RT candidates but ensure that only full-length LTR RTs are analyzed. Following filtering, the long terminal repeat regions of each transposable element were aligned, and the Jukes-Cantor model was used to estimate the divergence time of the two LTR regions. We used a mutation rate of 6.5âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> per site per year to estimate the years since insertion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella Genome and the Evolution of Flowering Plants. Science (80-.), 342, (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d58094e3036">16</a></sup>. This mutation rate is half that of rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404 LPâ12410 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-019-53968-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d58094e3040">36</a></sup> and is a general estimate; therefore, the insertion times should only be used in reference to the relative timing of insertion, rather than as exact dates.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>All of the raw reads and associated genomic and transcriptomic assemblies can be found in the NCBI BioProjects under PRJNA511033. All of the tissue used for sequencing came from one doubled haploid genotype (Voucher: M. Whitten #5841, University of Florida Herbarium, FLAS).</p>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/cgi-bin/nph-data_query?link_type=ABSTRACT&amp;bibcode=1998PNAS...95.2011B" aria-label="View reference 7 on ADS">ADS</a>Â
